# Building Decarbonization Retrofits for Commercial and Multifamily Buildings

## Introduction

Building decarbonization retrofits are crucial for reducing greenhouse gas emissions in commercial and multifamily buildings. These retrofits involve upgrading building systems and materials to reduce energy consumption and carbon emissions.

## Benefits of Building Decarbonization Retrofits

*   Reduces greenhouse gas emissions
*   Improves energy efficiency
*   Increases property value
*   Enhances occupant health and comfort

## Types of Building Decarbonization Retrofits

*   Lighting retrofits
*   HVAC system upgrades
*   Insulation and air sealing
*   Renewable energy systems
*   Energy management systems

## Case Studies

*   Example 1: A commercial building in California reduced its energy consumption by 30% through lighting retrofits and HVAC system upgrades.
*   Example 2: A multifamily building in New York City reduced its carbon emissions by 50% through insulation and air sealing, as well as the installation of a rooftop solar array.

## Conclusion

Building decarbonization retrofits are a critical step towards reducing greenhouse gas emissions in commercial and multifamily buildings. By upgrading building systems and materials, building owners and managers can reduce energy consumption, improve occupant health and comfort, and increase property value.

# Multifamily Buildings
Peachtree Corners

## Commercial Refrigeration Systems

### Overview

Commercial refrigeration systems are designed to provide efficient and reliable cooling for various applications, including supermarkets, restaurants, and other commercial establishments.

#### System Components

* Compressor: The compressor is the heart of the refrigeration system, responsible for compressing the refrigerant and transferring heat from the cold side to the hot side.
* Condenser: The condenser is a heat exchanger that dissipates heat from the hot refrigerant gas to the surrounding air or water.
* Expansion Valve: The expansion valve is a device that reduces the pressure of the refrigerant, allowing it to expand and become cold.
* Evaporator: The evaporator is a heat exchanger that absorbs heat from the surrounding air or water and transfers it to the refrigerant.

### System Performance

* **Temperature Range:** The commercial refrigeration system operates within a temperature range of 32°F to 40°F (0°C to 4°C).
* **Efficiency:** The system has an efficiency rating of 10.5 kW/ton, which is compliant with ASHRAE Standard 211.
* **Emission Factors:** The system emits 0.5 kg CO2/kWh, which is within the acceptable limits set by the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA).

### System Maintenance

* Regular maintenance is essential to ensure the system operates efficiently and effectively.
* The system should be inspected and cleaned every 6 months to prevent the buildup of dirt and debris.
* The compressor and condenser should be checked for leaks and replaced if necessary.

### System Upgrades

* The system can be upgraded to a more efficient model, such as a high-efficiency compressor or a variable-speed drive.
* The system can also be upgraded to a more environmentally friendly model, such as a system that uses a natural refrigerant.

### System Troubleshooting

* The system may experience issues such as low refrigerant levels, high compressor temperatures, or faulty sensors.
* The system should be checked for any blockages or leaks in the refrigerant lines.
* The system should also be checked for any issues with the compressor or condenser.

### System Codes and Standards

* The system complies with ASHRAE Standard 211 and the International Energy Conservation Code (IECC).
* The system also complies with the EPA's Energy Star program.

### System References

* ASHRAE Standard 211: Commercial Refrigeration Systems
* IECC: International Energy Conservation Code
* EPA: Environmental Protection Agency

### System Tables

| System Component | Efficiency Rating |
| --- | --- |
| Compressor | 10.5 kW/ton |
| Condenser | 8.5 kW/ton |
| Expansion Valve | 9.5 kW/ton |
| Evaporator | 11.5 kW/ton |

### System Figures

* Figure 1: Commercial Refrigeration System Schematic
* Figure 2: System Component Diagram

### System Bibliography

* Gunasekaran, R. (2026). Commercial Refrigeration Systems. Accuris.
* ASHRAE. (2026). ASHRAE Standard 211: Commercial Refrigeration Systems.

# Sustainable Buildings
## Buildings--Retrofitting
## Carbon dioxide mitigation

### Abstract

The book discusses sustainable buildings, retrofitting, and carbon dioxide mitigation. It covers various topics such as domestic hot water electrification, integration with other systems, heat recovery, thermal energy storage, control systems, equipment replacement cycle considerations, refrigerant considerations, life-cycle costs and financial incentives, cold-climate strategies, and non-HVAC considerations.

### Preface

The book is provided by the publisher and is copyrighted material licensed to Gunasekaran Raju on 2026-01-10 for licensee's use only.

### Identifiers

* LCCN: 2024007022
* ISBN: 9781955516754 (paperback)
* ISBN: 9781955516761 (PDF)

### Subjects

* Sustainable buildings
* Buildings--Retrofitting
* Carbon dioxide mitigation

### Classification

* LCC: TH880 .B877 2024
* DDC: 690.028/6--dc23/eng/20240523

### Table of Contents

* Chapter 1: Introduction
* Chapter 2: Domestic Hot Water Electrification
* Chapter 3: Integration with Other Systems
* Chapter 4: Heat Recovery
* Chapter 5: Thermal Energy Storage
* Chapter 6: Control Systems
* Chapter 7: Equipment Replacement Cycle Considerations
* Chapter 8: Refrigerant Considerations
* Chapter 9: Life-Cycle Costs and Financial Incentives
* Chapter 10: Cold-Climate Strategies
* Chapter 11: Non-HVAC Considerations

### Chapter 1: Introduction

The book discusses the importance of sustainable buildings and retrofitting in reducing carbon dioxide emissions. It highlights the need for domestic hot water electrification, integration with other systems, heat recovery, thermal energy storage, control systems, equipment replacement cycle considerations, refrigerant considerations, life-cycle costs and financial incentives, cold-climate strategies, and non-HVAC considerations.

### Chapter 2: Domestic Hot Water Electrification

Domestic hot water electrification is a key strategy in reducing carbon dioxide emissions. It involves the use of electric heat pumps or heat recovery systems to provide hot water.

### Chapter 3: Integration with Other Systems

Integration with other systems is crucial in achieving sustainable buildings. It involves the use of building management systems, energy management systems, and other technologies to optimize energy efficiency.

### Chapter 4: Heat Recovery

Heat recovery is a technique used to recover waste heat from various sources and use it to provide hot water or space heating.

### Chapter 5: Thermal Energy Storage

Thermal energy storage involves the use of phase change materials or other technologies to store thermal energy for later use.

### Chapter 6: Control Systems

Control systems play a crucial role in optimizing energy efficiency in buildings. They involve the use of sensors, actuators, and other technologies to control temperature, humidity, and other parameters.

### Chapter 7: Equipment Replacement Cycle Considerations

Equipment replacement cycle considerations involve the replacement of old equipment with new, energy-efficient equipment.

### Chapter 8: Refrigerant Considerations

Refrigerant considerations involve the selection of refrigerants that have low global warming potential.

### Chapter 9: Life-Cycle Costs and Financial Incentives

Life-cycle costs and financial incentives involve the calculation of the total cost of ownership of a building and the identification of financial incentives that can be used to reduce costs.

### Chapter 10: Cold-Climate Strategies

Cold-climate strategies involve the use of technologies such as heat pumps, solar thermal systems, and geothermal systems to provide space heating and hot water in cold climates.

### Chapter 11: Non-HVAC Considerations

Non-HVAC considerations involve the use of technologies such as load shifting, on-site renewables, electrical storage, and EV charging to reduce energy consumption.

### Conclusion

The book concludes by highlighting the importance of sustainable buildings and retrofitting in reducing carbon dioxide emissions. It emphasizes the need for a comprehensive approach that involves the use of various technologies and strategies to achieve energy efficiency.

# 4.4 Building Age and Envelope/Infrastructure Condition
Building age and envelope/infrastructure condition are critical factors in determining the energy efficiency of a building.

# 4.5 Space Constraints
Space constraints refer to the limitations imposed by the building's physical space, including the size and layout of the building.

# 4.6 Local Building Codes and Ordinances
Local building codes and ordinances are regulations that govern the construction and operation of buildings within a specific geographic area.

# 4.7 Historic Buildings and Regulatory Constraints
Historic buildings are subject to regulatory constraints that aim to preserve their historical and architectural integrity.

# 4.8 Occupant Considerations
Occupant considerations refer to the needs and preferences of the building's occupants, including factors such as comfort, health, and productivity.

# 4.9 Equity and Affordability
Equity and affordability refer to the fair distribution of resources and opportunities, including access to energy-efficient buildings.

## 4.9.1 Disadvantaged Communities
Disadvantaged communities are those that face significant barriers to accessing energy-efficient buildings and technologies.

## 4.9.2 Policies and Programs
Policies and programs are initiatives that aim to promote energy efficiency and equity in disadvantaged communities.

## 4.10 Building Owner Type
Building owner type refers to the type of entity that owns the building, including residential, commercial, or government entities.

## 4.10.1 Building Owner Occupied
Building owner occupied refers to buildings that are owned and occupied by the same entity.

## 4.10.2 Leased Buildings
Leased buildings are those that are rented or leased to tenants, often with a separate entity owning the building.

# 5 DECARBONIZATION STRATEGIES AND TECHNOLOGIES: BUILDING ELECTRICAL, HEATING AND COOLING LOAD REDUCTION
Decarbonization strategies and technologies aim to reduce the energy consumption of buildings through various means.

## 5.1 Electrical Load Reduction
Electrical load reduction refers to the strategies and technologies used to decrease the energy consumption of electrical systems in buildings.

## 5.1.1 Building Lighting Systems
Building lighting systems refer to the design and installation of lighting systems in buildings, including energy-efficient options.

## 5.1.2 Multifamily Kitchen Appliances and Cooking Systems
Multifamily kitchen appliances and cooking systems refer to the design and installation of kitchen appliances and cooking systems in multifamily buildings.

## 5.1.3 Other Systems and Plug Loads
Other systems and plug loads refer to the design and installation of electrical systems and plug loads in buildings.

## 5.2 Heating/Cooling Load Reduction: Building Envelope and Passive Systems
Heating/cooling load reduction refers to the strategies and technologies used to decrease the energy consumption of heating and cooling systems in buildings.

## 5.2.1 Building Envelope
Building envelope refers to the exterior components of a building, including walls, windows, and doors.

## 5.2.2 Passive Strategies
Passive strategies refer to design and construction techniques that reduce energy consumption without the use of active systems.

## 5.2.3 HVAC Optimization
HVAC optimization refers to the use of advanced technologies and techniques to optimize the performance of heating, ventilation, and air conditioning systems.

# 8 DECARBONIZATION STRATEGIES AND TECHNOLOGIES: DOMESTIC HOT-WATER SYSTEMS
### 8.1 Evaluating Existing Conditions
#### 8.1.1 System Configuration and Condition
The existing domestic hot-water (DHW) system configuration and condition play a crucial role in determining the decarbonization strategies and technologies that can be implemented.

#### 8.1.2 DHW Load Profile
The DHW load profile is a critical factor in evaluating the existing conditions of a DHW system. It is essential to understand the peak and off-peak demand patterns to determine the most suitable decarbonization strategy.

### 8.2 Load Reduction and Rightsizing
Load reduction and rightsizing are essential strategies for reducing the energy consumption of a DHW system.

#### 8.2.1 Low-Flow Fixture Retrofit
Low-flow fixture retrofit is a cost-effective strategy for reducing the energy consumption of a DHW system. By installing low-flow fixtures, the hot water demand can be reduced, resulting in lower energy consumption.

#### 8.2.2 Distribution and Storage Losses
Distribution and storage losses are significant contributors to the energy consumption of a DHW system. By implementing strategies to reduce these losses, the energy consumption of the system can be significantly reduced.

#### 8.2.3 Passive Heat Recovery (Drain-Line Heat Recovery)
Passive heat recovery, also known as drain-line heat recovery, is a strategy that involves recovering heat from the drain line of a DHW system. This heat can be used to preheat the incoming cold water, reducing the energy consumption of the system.

#### 8.2.4 Rightsizing
Rightsizing involves sizing the DHW system to meet the actual hot water demand of the building. This can result in significant energy savings by reducing the energy consumption of the system.

#### 8.2.5 Sizing Methodology for Storage-Based Systems
The sizing methodology for storage-based systems involves determining the required storage tank size based on the hot water demand of the building.

#### 8.2.6 Sizing Methodology for Point-of-Use Systems
The sizing methodology for point-of-use systems involves determining the required system size based on the hot water demand of the building.

#### 8.2.7 Sizing Considerations for Grid-Demand Response
Sizing considerations for grid-demand response involve determining the required system size based on the grid demand response requirements.

### 8.3 Distributed vs. Central Systems
Distributed vs. central systems is a critical consideration in determining the decarbonization strategies and technologies that can be implemented.

#### 8.3.1 Distributed DHW Systems
Distributed DHW systems involve installing multiple small storage tanks throughout the building. This can result in significant energy savings by reducing the energy consumption of the system.

#### 8.3.2 Central DHW Systems
Central DHW systems involve installing a single large storage tank in a central location. This can result in significant energy savings by reducing the energy consumption of the system.

#### 8.3.3 Application Considerations
Application considerations involve determining the most suitable decarbonization strategy based on the specific application and building requirements.

### 8.4 Recirculation Systems
Recirculation systems involve circulating hot water through the pipes to reduce energy losses.

#### 8.4.1 Reducing Recirculation Energy Losses
Reducing recirculation energy losses involves implementing strategies to reduce the energy consumption of the recirculation system.

#### 8.4.2 Direct-Return Systems
Direct-return systems involve circulating hot water directly back to the storage tank. This can result in significant energy savings by reducing the energy consumption of the system.

#### 8.4.3 Recirculation System Considerations for Central Single-Pass HPWHs
Recirculation system considerations for central single-pass HPWHs involve determining the most suitable recirculation strategy based on the specific application and building requirements.

### 8.5 Heat Pump Water Heaters
Heat pump water heaters involve using a heat pump to heat the water. This can result in significant energy savings by reducing the energy consumption of the system.

#### 8.5.1 Heat Pump Types
Heat pump types involve determining the most suitable heat pump type based on the specific application and building requirements.

### 8.6 Conclusion
In conclusion, decarbonization strategies and technologies for domestic hot-water systems involve evaluating existing conditions, load reduction and rightsizing, distributed vs. central systems, recirculation systems, and heat pump water heaters.

### 8.7 References
ASHRAE Standard 211, "Standard for Commercial and Industrial Fan-Powered and Gravity-Driven Hydronic Heating and Cooling Systems"

### 8.8 Index

# Decarbonization of Existing Building Stock

**Introduction**

1.1 Background
In response to the rising awareness of decarbonization's vital role in confronting climate change, the ASHRAE Position Document on Building Decarbonization was published stating ASHRAE's commitment to pursuing efforts related to building decarbonization. Applied to existing buildings, decarbonization is the reduction of carbon emissions through converting existing equipment and systems powered by fossil fuel combustion processes to highly efficient equipment and systems that use low-carbon or carbon-free fuels. It is the intent of this building decarbonization retrofit guide for existing commercial and multifamily buildings to serve as an educational resource on the processes of planning and implementing decarbonization efforts and to provide high-level technical guidance on available decarbonization technologies and strategies.

1.1.1 Why Decarbonization?
In 2020, existing buildings were responsible for nearly 40% of energy-related greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions, quantified as carbon dioxide equivalent (CO2-eq), as shown in Figure 1.1. In ASHRAE Position Document on Building Decarbonization, ASHRAE established that the global built environment must halve its 2015 GHG emissions levels by 2030. It lists three main pillars of this plan:

• all new buildings are net-zero GHG emissions in operation,
• widespread energy-efficiency retrofits of existing assets are well underway, and
• embodied carbon of new construction is reduced by at least 40% (ASHRAE 2022h).

**ASHRAE Position Document on Building Decarbonization**
This position document emphasizes the necessity of decarbonization, addresses key carbon-reduction principles and decarbonization strategies, and poses challenges that need to be addressed (ASHRAE 2022h). As society faces the challenge of mitigating climate change, ASHRAE's position is that the decarbonization of buildings and their systems must be based on a holistic analysis that includes healthy, safe, and comfortable environments; energy efficiency; environmental impacts; sustainability; operational security; and economics.

---

**ASHRAE Public Policy Information Brief on Building Electrification**

Building electrification is often viewed as an essential strategy for building decarbonization, but electrification, in and of itself, does not necessarily guarantee decarbonization. Existing buildings' ability to electrify cost effectively will accelerate with technological improvements and local and national policy incentives. ASHRAE supports the global need to reduce emissions from buildings, including through beneficial electrification. This public policy information brief (PPIB) outlines the issues and considerations for policy makers regarding building electrification (ASHRAE 2023d).

**Decarbonizing an existing building is the process of reducing its carbon emissions through the building's life cycle. Generally, building construction, energy use, methane leakage, and refrigerants are the primary sources of GHG emissions (ASHRAE 2022h). This guide offers mitigation approaches such as building electrification, energy efficiency and demand management, embodied carbon considerations, and more for the various sources of building carbon emissions. Chapter 2 describes what can be found in each chapter and how to use this guide.**

**1.1.2.1 Types of Carbon in Existing Buildings**

There are two types of carbon in existing buildings: operational carbon and embodied carbon. Embodied carbon comes from material extraction, manufacturing processes, transport, refrigerant leakage, and waste processing, while operational carbon is generated as the building uses energy and water during its operations.

**Figure 1.1 Energy-related CO2 emissions in 2020. (Image credit: ASHRAE 2022a)**

| **All Sectors** | **Buildings, 37%** | **Residential (Indirect), 30%** | **Residential (Direct), 16%** | **Non-Residential (Indirect), 19%** | **Non-Residential (Direct), 8%** | **Building Construction (Embodied), 27%** |

**ASHRAE Public Policy Information Brief on Building Electrification**

Building electrification is often viewed as an essential strategy for building decarbonization, but electrification, in and of itself, does not necessarily guarantee decarbonization. Existing buildings' ability to electrify cost effectively will accelerate with technological improvements and local and national policy incentives. ASHRAE supports the global need to reduce emissions from buildings, including through beneficial electrification. This public policy information brief (PPIB) outlines the issues and considerations for policy makers regarding building electrification (ASHRAE 2023d).

**Decarbonizing an existing building is the process of reducing its carbon emissions through the building's life cycle. Generally, building construction, energy use, methane leakage, and refrigerants are the primary sources of GHG emissions (ASHRAE 2022h). This guide offers mitigation approaches such as building electrification, energy efficiency and demand management, embodied carbon considerations, and more for the various sources of building carbon emissions. Chapter 2 describes what can be found in each chapter and how to use this guide.**

**1.1.2.1 Types of Carbon in Existing Buildings**

There are two types of carbon in existing buildings: operational carbon and embodied carbon. Embodied carbon comes from material extraction, manufacturing processes, transport, refrigerant leakage, and waste processing, while operational carbon is generated as the building uses energy and water during its operations.

## Figure: Building Whole-Life Carbon Stages

This figure illustrates the various stages of a building's life cycle, highlighting the different types of carbon emissions associated with each stage. The key finding is that embodied carbon, which is the carbon dioxide equivalent of the energy used to produce and transport building materials, is a significant contributor to a building's overall carbon footprint.

### Data Representation

*   **Embodied Carbon**: This stage includes the production, transportation, and installation of building materials, which can account for up to 50% of a building's total carbon emissions.
*   **Operational Carbon**: This stage includes the energy used to operate and maintain the building, which can account for up to 30% of a building's total carbon emissions.
*   **End-of-Life Carbon**: This stage includes the demolition, deconstruction, and disposal of the building, which can account for up to 20% of a building's total carbon emissions.

### Metric

*   **Carbon Emissions**: The figure measures the carbon emissions associated with each stage of a building's life cycle.

### Chart Type

*   **Bar Chart**: The figure uses a bar chart to compare the different stages of a building's life cycle and their associated carbon emissions.

### Units

*   **Tonnes of CO2e**: The figure measures carbon emissions in tonnes of CO2e (carbon dioxide equivalent).

### Chart Generation Hint

*   **X-axis**: The x-axis represents the different stages of a building's life cycle, including embodied carbon, operational carbon, and end-of-life carbon.
*   **Y-axis**: The y-axis represents the carbon emissions associated with each stage, measured in tonnes of CO2e.
*   **Key Threshold Lines**: The figure includes key threshold lines to highlight the different stages of a building's life cycle and their associated carbon emissions.
*   **Annotations**: The figure includes annotations to provide additional context and information about each stage of a building's life cycle.
*   **Colour Coding**: The figure uses colour coding to differentiate between the different stages of a building's life cycle and their associated carbon emissions.

|                                                                                                     |
| --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| Figure 1.2 Building whole-life carbon stages. (Image credit: P2S Inc., figure based on CE N
[2011]) |

# Introduction
## Figure: Decarbonization Strategies
Decarbonization strategies aim to reduce greenhouse gas emissions from existing buildings, mitigating climate change impacts such as extreme heat, intensifying storms, and unhealthy air quality, while promoting improved occupant health and well-being through increased indoor air quality.

### Data Representation
- **Reduced Operating Costs**: Building operating costs may decrease due to more efficient equipment, rightsizing building systems, on-site renewable generation, and other conservation measures.
- **Mitigation of Climate Change Impacts**: ASHRAE recognizes the need to reduce GHG emissions from existing buildings to mitigate the impacts of climate change, including climate-caused disasters and displacements.
- **Improved Occupant Health and Well-Being**: Well-designed decarbonized building systems promote increased indoor air quality (IAQ) through weatherization, proper ventilation, and the elimination of nitrogen dioxide, carbon monoxide, and other chemical emissions generated on-site.

### Chart Generation Hint
To recreate:
- **X-axis**: Label and categories not provided.
- **Y-axis**: Label and range not provided.
- **Key Threshold Lines, Annotations, or Colour Coding**: Not applicable.
- **Baseline or Reference Value**: Not applicable.

# Building Decarbonization Retrofits for Commercial and Multifamily Buildings

## Community Economic Benefits

Significant investments are being made to grow a workforce skilled in building decarbonization. Design consultants, contractors, building officials, and facility managers are just a few of the roles that will require new skills and increased numbers to meet the demands of emerging decarbonization policy. The need for an upskilled and increased workforce may result in a greater number of higher-quality jobs. Climate-related disasters and mitigation are also a significant cost to communities. Disaster-related recovery costs often result in the displacement of community members.

## 1.2 Existing Building Decarbonization in Building Codes and Ordinances

Building energy codes have focused primarily on reducing energy consumption for new construction buildings and major renovations and on addressing alterations when equipment or building components are replaced by the building owner. However, a growing number of jurisdictions, such as Denver, New York City, Vancouver, and the State of Washington, have realized that existing building decarbonization must be part of the strategy to meet their emissions reduction goals. As a result, ordinances have become increasingly popular requiring existing building owners to report their consumption through building benchmarking and, in some instances, reduce it through building performance standards (BPS). ASHRAE has created a companion guide on building performance standards to address the policy aspect of achieving existing building decarbonization.

## Building Performance Standards: A Technical Resource Guide

This guide is intended to provide a technical basis and resources to policy makers, building owners, facility managers, design professionals, and ASHRAE members when developing and implementing building performance standards (BPS) (ASHRAE 2023g). The guide focuses on larger building types and scope of BPS as developed by leading U.S. cities and states. The guide covers BPS aimed at reducing building operating energy use and resulting emissions and does not cover embodied energy or carbon, which are addressed by the Whole-Life Carbon Guide for Building Systems.

| Building
Performance Standards
A Technical Resource Guide |
| --------------------------------------------------------- |
| ASHRAE
U.S. Department of Energy                          |

# How to Use This Guide

## 2.1 Purpose and Organization

This guide is organized to take the reader on a path through a decarbonization project, includ- ing goal setting, planning, identifying strategies, implementation, metric tracking, financing, and specific considerations for existing buildings. Each chapter contains actionable steps and resources. Case studies of real projects are referenced throughout the guide to illustrate suggested procedures, systems, and technology with real success stories and lessons learned. The guide intends to encompass the best practices for developing and conducting decarbonization retrofits for commercial and multifamily buildings.

## Chapter 3 lays out the path for decarbonizing an existing building. It covers the planning process, including goal setting and stakeholder engagement, how to conduct a decarbonization assessment, determining appropriate decarbonization measures, drafting an implementation plan, implementing the plan, and tracking metrics.

## Chapter 4 addresses constraints such as structural capacity and available space, infrastructure conditions, renovating an occupied building, financing, local building codes and ordinances, and buildings with district systems.

## Figure: Electrical Systems and Heating and Cooling Load Reductions

This figure presents a detailed breakdown of the components involved in electrical systems and heating and cooling load reductions. The key finding is that it offers strategies for reducing electrical loads, including those from lighting systems and appliances, through building envelope adjustments, HVAC system existing building commissioning (i.e., retro- or recommissioning), and HVAC energy recovery.

### Data Representation

*   **Substrate**: The base material of the building envelope.
*   **Adhesive**: The material used to bond the substrate and other components together.
*   **Insulation**: The material used to reduce heat transfer between the inside and outside of the building.
*   **Weatherproofing Membrane**: The material used to prevent water from entering the building through the roof or walls.
*   **Building Envelope**: The outer layer of the building that separates the interior from the exterior environment.
*   **HVAC System**: The heating, ventilation, and air conditioning system that provides thermal comfort to the occupants.
*   **Retro- or Recommissioning**: The process of optimizing the performance of an existing HVAC system.
*   **HVAC Energy Recovery**: The process of recovering energy from the HVAC system to reduce energy consumption.

### Chart Generation Hint

To recreate this figure, the following steps can be taken:

*   **X-axis**: Label and categories for the different components involved in electrical systems and heating and cooling load reductions.
*   **Y-axis**: Label and range for the different values represented in the figure.
*   **Key Threshold Lines**: Annotations or color coding to highlight important information, such as the optimal insulation thickness or the most efficient HVAC system configuration.
*   **Baseline or Reference Value**: A line or value to serve as a reference point for comparison, such as the baseline energy consumption of a typical building.

By following these steps, a similar figure can be created to illustrate the strategies for reducing electrical loads and improving heating and cooling efficiency in buildings.

Insulated, prefabricated metal panels with energy-efficient windows at building • Prefabricated panels included electrical infrastructure and punchouts for PTHP upgrades, including capacity for future building electrification • Installation of PTHPs at one building with central ventilation upgrades and centralized controls • Common area dryer electrification at one building • VRF installation in common areas of two buildings • DHW electrification using centralized heat pump water heaters at one building • Submetering apartment electrical use at all three buildings for accurate tenant billing • Tenant engagement and education plan to understand purpose and operation of upgrades |

This table presents a comprehensive overview of building decarbonization retrofits for commercial and multifamily buildings, highlighting various strategies and technologies employed to reduce energy consumption and greenhouse gas emissions. The table is divided into six columns, each representing a different building location, and six rows, each detailing a specific retrofit measure.

*   McMullen County Courthouse
*   Ken Soble Tower

*   LED Lighting and Window Retrofits
*   Insulation Upgrades
*   Heat Recovery and DOAS
*   Parking Roof PV
*   Triple-Pane Window Replacements
*   Central Boiler Hydronic Baseboards
*   DOAS with ERVs
*   Condensing Boiler for DHW
*   LED Lighting Retrofit with Occupancy Controls
*   Converted Pneumatic to DDC
*   VAV Terminal Units
*   Chiller/Boiler Plant Replacement
*   Window Replacement
*   Weatherization
*   Attic Air Sealing and Insulation
*   Replaced Gas-Fired Packaged RTUs
*   PTHP and Ducted Split Heat Pump
*   Distributed HPWHs
*   Carport PV with Virtual Net Metering
*   LED Lighting Retrofit with Controls
*   Window Replacements and Weatherization
*   Converted Pneumatic to DDC
*   Replaced Chiller/Boiler Plant
*   GSHP Well Field Sizing

### Key Findings

*   The table highlights the importance of a multi-faceted approach to building decarbonization, incorporating various retrofit measures to reduce energy consumption and greenhouse gas emissions.
*   The use of LED lighting and window retrofits, insulation upgrades, and heat recovery systems can significantly reduce energy consumption and emissions.
*   The implementation of parking roof PV, triple-pane window replacements, and central boiler hydronic baseboards can also contribute to energy savings and emissions reduction.
*   The table emphasizes the need for a comprehensive approach to building decarbonization, incorporating multiple retrofit measures to achieve significant energy savings and emissions reduction.

### Data Representation

*   The table presents data in a clear and concise manner, making it easy to compare and contrast different retrofit measures and their impact on energy consumption and emissions.
*   The use of bullet points and short sentences makes the information easy to read and understand.

*   Use charts and graphs to visualize the data and highlight key trends and patterns.

    *   kWh (kilowatt-hours)

    *   % (percentage)

*   The use of bullet points and short sentences makes the information easy to read and understand.

*   The y-axis is labeled with the retrofit measure name and description.

# Case Study Specifications (Continued)
=====================================

## Stella B. Werner Council Office Building
----------------------------------------

*   LED lighting retrofit reduced electric demand
*   Window replacements reduced heating/cooling loads
*   Mixed HVAC equipment types due to multiple renovations
*   Replaced HVAC systems (chillers/boilers, RTUs, FCUs, WSHPs) with 300-ton VRF with heat recovery and DOAS
*   VRF refrigerant lines easier to implement with limited ceiling height

## Virginia Beach City Public Schools
------------------------------------

*   Phased project across multiple schools
*   Weatherization measures
*   For each school, installed central GSHP serving WSHP loop
*   Replaced packaged RTUs with GSHP RTUs

## STAR Transit
----------------

*   Bus fleet conversion to electric buses
*   Planned installation of PV
*   Evaluation demonstrating importance of BESS to align PV production with overnight bus fleet charging

## North Oak Development
----------------------

*   District ambient loop for new development
*   Ground source loop provides space heating/cooling for buildings
*   Modular design allows future buildings to be connected
*   DHW loads served by separate boiler

## Kitsilano Pool
-----------------

*   40% DHW load reduction from low-flow fixtures
*   After load reduction, central DHW system retrofit with CO2 ASHP and two 250-gallon storage tanks

# Section 3.3, Perform Decarbonization Assessment
Assessing the building will identify opportunities and constraints that must be considered specific to the existing building.

# Section 3.4, Prioritize/Determine Decarbonization Measures
Prioritizing and determining decarbonization measures is typically done as a part of the decarbonization assessment. Several aspects must be considered when determining the right package of measures to meet decarbonization goals. The assessment results will inform measure selection.

# Section 3.5, Develop a Building Decarbonization Plan
Once the measures have been identified, an actionable plan should be detailed to create a playbook for the specific building, including considerations such as limiting disruptions to occupants, etc.

# Section 3.6, Implement the Building Decarbonization Plan
Implementing the decarbonization plan moves the building down the path toward meeting its decarbonization goals.

# Section 3.7, Track and Report Performance
Performance metrics must be tracked to demonstrate progress toward goals and eventual goal achievement, and to update the decarbonization plan as needed.

## 3.2 Decarbonization Planning
The first step to decarbonizing a building is to set the framework for decarbonization. Depending on the magnitude of desired emissions reduction, this can be a multiyear process.

### 3.2.1 Setting Decarbonization Goals
Decarbonization goals should be set based on the building's current emissions profile and desired reduction targets. This will inform the development of a decarbonization plan.

### 3.2.2 Identifying Decarbonization Measures
Decarbonization measures should be identified based on the building's energy usage patterns, existing infrastructure, and available technologies. This will inform the development of a decarbonization plan.

### 3.2.3 Developing a Decarbonization Plan
A decarbonization plan should be developed based on the identified decarbonization measures and goals. This plan should include a roadmap for implementation, timelines, and responsible parties.

### 3.2.4 Implementing Decarbonization Measures
Decarbonization measures should be implemented in accordance with the decarbonization plan. This may involve upgrades to existing systems, installation of new technologies, or changes to operational practices.

### 3.2.5 Monitoring and Evaluating Progress
Progress toward decarbonization goals should be monitored and evaluated regularly. This will inform adjustments to the decarbonization plan as needed.

## 3.3.1 ASHRAE Standard 211
ASHRAE Standard 211 provides guidelines for the design and operation of HVAC systems to minimize energy consumption and emissions.

## 3.3.2 Energy Efficiency Measures
Energy efficiency measures, such as LED lighting and energy-efficient HVAC systems, can significantly reduce energy consumption and emissions.

## 3.3.3 Renewable Energy Systems
Renewable energy systems, such as solar and wind power, can provide a significant portion of a building's energy needs and reduce emissions.

## 3.3.4 Energy Storage Systems
Energy storage systems, such as batteries, can help to stabilize the grid and reduce emissions by storing excess energy generated by renewable sources.

## 3.3.5 Electric Vehicle Charging Infrastructure
Electric vehicle charging infrastructure can support the adoption of electric vehicles and reduce emissions by providing a convenient and accessible charging solution.

## 3.3.6 Building Automation Systems
Building automation systems can optimize energy consumption and reduce emissions by providing real-time monitoring and control of building systems.

## 3.3.7 Commissioning and Testing
Commissioning and testing of building systems can ensure that they are operating efficiently and effectively, and that any issues are identified and addressed promptly.

## 3.3.8 Maintenance and Operations
Regular maintenance and operations of building systems can help to ensure that they continue to operate efficiently and effectively, and that any issues are identified and addressed promptly.

## 3.3.9 Training and Education
Training and education of building operators and maintenance personnel can help to ensure that they have the knowledge and skills needed to operate and maintain building systems efficiently and effectively.

## 3.3.10 Performance Metrics
Performance metrics, such as energy consumption and emissions, should be tracked and reported regularly to demonstrate progress toward decarbonization goals.

## 3.3.11 Reporting and Verification
Reporting and verification of decarbonization progress should be conducted regularly to ensure that goals are being met and to identify areas for improvement.

## 3.3.12 Continuous Improvement
Continuous improvement of decarbonization strategies and measures should be pursued to ensure that goals are being met and to identify areas for improvement.

## 3.3.13 Stakeholder Engagement
Stakeholder engagement and communication should be conducted regularly to ensure that all parties are informed and engaged in the decarbonization process.

## 3.3.14 Community Outreach
Community outreach and education should be conducted regularly to raise awareness of decarbonization efforts and to engage the community in the process.

## 3.3.15 Public Awareness
Public awareness and education should be conducted regularly to raise awareness of decarbonization efforts and to engage the public in the process.

## 3.3.16 Media Outreach
Media outreach and communication should be conducted regularly to raise awareness of decarbonization efforts and to engage the media in the process.

## 3.3.17 Social Media
Social media should be used to raise awareness of decarbonization efforts and to engage the public in the process.

## 3.3.18 Website
A website should be maintained to provide information on decarbonization efforts and to engage the public in the process.

## 3.3.19 Decarbonization Plan
A decarbonization plan should be developed and implemented to guide decarbonization efforts and to ensure that goals are being met.

## 3.3.20 Decarbonization Metrics
Decarbonization metrics, such as energy consumption and emissions, should be tracked and reported regularly to demonstrate progress toward decarbonization goals.

## 3.3.21 Decarbonization Reporting
Decarbonization reporting and verification should be conducted regularly to ensure that goals are being met and to identify areas for improvement.

## 3.3.22 Decarbonization Continuous Improvement

# Chapter 3 Path for Existing Building Decarbonization

## Establish Framework

* Set Goals & Baseline
* Define Timeline
* Define Scope
* Engage Stakeholders

## Prioritize Measures

* Evaluate/Reduce Loads
* Maximize Efficiency
* Electrify
* Optimize Controls
* Consider Generation and Storage
* Calculate Carbon Impact
* Package Measures
* Determine Financing

## Perform Assessment

* Decarbonization Focus
* Evaluation Tools

## Draft Decarbonization Plan

* Summarize Measures and Triggers
* Project Emissions
* Determine Infrastructure Impacts

## Implement Decarbonization Plan

* Minimize Operational Disruptions
* Monitor Systems
* Communicate with Occupants
* Plan Site Logistics

## Track and Report Performance

* Measure Installed Performance
* Measure Ongoing Performance
* Perform O&M Procedures

# 3.2.1 Set Decarbonization Goals

Building Performance Standards: A Technical Resource Guide

This guide is intended to provide a technical basis and resources to policy makers, building owners, facility managers, design professionals, and ASHRAE members when developing and implementing building performance standards (BPS) (ASHRAE 2023g). The guide focuses on larger building types and scope of BPS as developed by leading U.S. cities and states. The guide covers BPS aimed at reducing building operating energy use and resulting emissions and does not cover embodied energy or carbon, which are addressed by the Whole-Life Carbon Guide for Building Systems.

The critical first step of a decarbonization plan is to establish goals with buy-in from building ownership and stakeholders.

Goals may be based on regulatory requirements, corporate goals, tenant requests, or other driving factors. Building performance standards are one such goal-setting mechanism employed by some governments. See Building Performance Standards: A Technical Resource Guide for detailed discussion on these ordinances and how they may affect existing building decarbonization goals. Goals may be chosen by a specific building or portfolio based on ownership or stakeholder interests, such as net zero by 2050, and may also include interim goals, such as 30% emissions reduction by 2030 compared to performance in 2015.

Setting decarbonization goals may coincide with a GHG inventory or GHG baseline determination to set specific performance targets or as part of a larger portfolio prioritization effort.

## CASE STUDY: University of California, Santa Cruz—Phasing Approach

In pursuit of UC Santa Cruz’s and the broader UC system’s goals, UCSC Chancellor Cindy Larive created a diverse Decarbonization and Electrification Task Force and charged them with researching the feasibility of reducing UCSC’s Scope 1 and 2 carbon emissions, ideally to zero, by 2030 or as soon as possible. See the UCSC Phasing Approach case study in the appendix for details on the decarbonization actions taken by UC Santa Cruz.

# 3.2.2 Define Timeline

The planning process should include establishing a timeline for implementing measures in meeting the building’s decarbonization targets. The timeline may be up to the building owner, or it may be dictated by an incentive program or local ordinance. Even if timelines-to-meet targets are set, there is still flexibility when deciding which decarbonization measures to implement and when.

An important aspect of defining a timeline includes establishing a capital improvements budget. Minor decarbonization upgrades might be funded directly from the building’s operation and maintenance budget, but buildings that need significant improvements—or whole-building portfolios or campuses—may require more extensive planning and budgeting. As a result, a multiyear process may be required to determine the decarbonization strategy, outline required investment through contractor bids, and acquire the necessary funds.

For facilities that have aging systems approaching the end of their effective useful life (EUL) or requiring persistent maintenance, planning for more efficient and electric replacement options is valuable when establishing a decarbonization timeline. In current practice, equipment is often replaced upon failure and requires immediate replacement. Not having adequate electrical and/or 16

---

# CASE STUDY: University of California, Santa Cruz—Phasing Approach
The University of California Santa Cruz decided on a phased approach to decarbonization for their campus. The plan implemented interim solutions when existing gas boilers were due for replacement. When a building had insufficient electrical capacity to support immediate installation of an air-source heat pump system, a condensing boiler replacement was chosen to lower direct emissions in the interim until sufficient electrical capacity or district heating was available to fully decarbonize the heating system. See the UCSC Phasing Approach case study in the appendix for more detail.

Other timeline considerations include taking advantage of available financial incentives (e.g., utility, municipal, state, federal) and impact on building occupants. Many incentives have a sunset date, intending to motivate customers to implement projects sooner than later.

Timing capital improvement projects to minimize the impact on occupants is a standard practice of existing building upgrades and being prepared to replace lighting or appliances during tenant renovations, for example, can be included in the building's decarbonization timeline. Any facility that has a decarbonization goal, even if the due date to meet a target is well in the future, will benefit from developing a timeline as part of the planning process. Performing an assessment, determining measures, implementing measures, and collecting data for reporting may take several years, and these later steps in the process will benefit from cataloging timeline considerations during planning.

# 3.2.3 Define Scope and Level of Decarbonization Assessment
Together with defining the project timeline, determining the project scope is an important exercise when starting your decarbonization project. Scope decisions are often driven by the established performance targets and by economic feasibility. Additional factors may also play a role, such as equity considerations, resilience planning, seismic upgrades and preparedness, public relations, or impacts to the timeline. For tenant-occupied buildings, decarbonization measures likely will involve tenant systems requiring updates to design and operation requirements and potentially lease agreements. More on considerations regarding tenant-occupied buildings is included in Section 4.10.

# CASE STUDY: 345 Hudson, New York
The case study for 345 Hudson defined scope and goals early. These were:

• Net zero by 2030
• Fossil-fuel-free heating
• 30% to 40% reduction in energy use
• Compliance with NYC Local Law 97

Another key item was minimizing disruptions to tenants. These goals drove the evaluation and selection of measures and implementation of a modular project that allowed phasing to minimize disruption.

---

# 3.2.4 Identify and Inform Stakeholders
The stakeholders in an organization typically include the investors/owners, employees, customers (people paying for the business products/services, using the facility, or paying local taxes), and suppliers, all of whom have a vested interest in the organization’s performance.

## Table 3.1 Stakeholder Categories and Responsibilities

| Stakeholder Category | Responsibilities |
| --- | --- |
| Investors/Owners | Pay for the decarbonization project, pass the cost along to customers |
| Employees | Critical to the project’s long-term success, need special expertise to maintain the new system(s) |
| Customers | Experience the economic impact of the decarbonization project |
| Facilities Managers | Maintain the new system(s), ensure that intended savings persist over time |
| Suppliers | Provide necessary resources for the decarbonization project |

The World Resource Institute’s Greenhouse Gas Protocol is often referenced when doing GHG emissions accounting, which breaks emissions down into direct emissions (Scope 1), indirect emissions (Scope 2), and all other emissions (Scope 3). The WRI GHG Protocol is not specific to the building industry, however, and ASHRAE has proposed Standards 240P and 242P to address the categorization of building-specific emissions. At the time of publication, this guide recommends categorization based on EN 15978 (CEN 2011), as referenced in Section 1.1.2.1 in Chapter 1, rather than the GHG Protocol.

## Building Occupant Transportation Emissions

The Greenhouse Gas Protocol is often referenced when doing GHG emissions accounting, which breaks emissions down into direct emissions (Scope 1), indirect emissions (Scope 2), and all other emissions (Scope 3). The WRI GHG Protocol is not specific to the building industry, however, and ASHRAE has proposed Standards 240P and 242P to address the categorization of building-specific emissions.

## ASHRAE Standards 240P and 242P

ASHRAE has proposed Standards 240P and 242P to address the categorization of building-specific emissions. At the time of publication, this guide recommends categorization based on EN 15978 (CEN 2011), as referenced in Section 1.1.2.1 in Chapter 1, rather than the GHG Protocol.

## Categorization of Building-Specific Emissions

The categorization of building-specific emissions is based on EN 15978 (CEN 2011), which breaks emissions down into direct emissions (Scope 1), indirect emissions (Scope 2), and all other emissions (Scope 3). The WRI GHG Protocol is not specific to the building industry, however, and ASHRAE has proposed Standards 240P and 242P to address the categorization of building-specific emissions.

## Decarbonization Planning

Decarbonization planning involves identifying and informing stakeholders, including investors/owners, employees, customers, and suppliers. The stakeholders in an organization typically have a vested interest in the organization’s performance and should be informed and approved the project, along with any employed facilities support staff.

## Facilities Support Staff

Facilities support staff are critical to the project’s long-term success and should be included in early discussions to ensure that intended savings persist over time. Gathering input from facilities support staff is an important step because they are critical to the project’s long-term success. Ensuring that they understand the project’s goals and the need to change systems or operations will pave the path for efficient building operation after the decarbonization project is completed.

---

# Decarbonizing Existing Buildings
## Equity Considerations During Stakeholder Engagement

Decarbonizing existing buildings may have social and economic impacts, such as changes in property values, adjustments in rental rates and agreements, job creation, and community development. Building owners and managers should consider these factors when planning decarbonization efforts and work with stakeholders to ensure that they are aligned with community goals and needs.

### 3.2.4.1 Equity Considerations During Stakeholder Engagement

When buildings upgrades are required to meet performance targets, such as when there is a local building performance standard ordinance, additional support is often offered to address equity considerations. If the building is in a disadvantaged community or has unique equity circumstances, check with the local jurisdiction for additional resources available and for expectations around stakeholder engagement.

### Stakeholder Engagement

The following stakeholders should be considered during the decarbonization planning process:

| Stakeholder Category                                                | Stakeholder Responsibilities                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| Executive Leadership                                                | Provide resources and support to effectively develop and implement the plan (e.g., CEO, CFO, COO).                                                                                                                                                                              |
| Finance                                                             | Identify the financial metrics required to assess the business case and provide guidance on potential financing mechanisms.                                                                                                                                                     |
| Property Management, Facilities/Engineering, and Energy Management | Identify operational needs and maintenance concerns, suggest and evaluate technical solutions, and provide building-level information.                                                                                                                                          |
| Capital Planning, Procurement, and Project Management               | Identify workload expectations and opportunities to streamline procurement and project-delivery efforts when implementing activities at scale.                                                                                                                                  |
| ESG/Sustainability                                                  | Identify sustainability considerations and expectations, including those beyond operational carbon emissions. Provide input on the risks and opportunities of the GHG Emissions Reduction Plan and review the plan through a lens of ESG regulatory and reporting requirements. |
| Real Estate and Transaction                                         | Provide insight into the organization’s future space needs and incorporate decarbonization requirements into considerations for new construction or acquisitions.                                                                                                               |
| Marketing                                                           | Identify ways to communicate efforts and GHG emissions reduction planning status within and beyond the organization.                                                                                                                                                            |
| Occupants and Community Members (External)                          | Identify ways to address occupant concerns and improve their overall experience through emissions reduction measures. Consider opportunities to collaborate with the broader community on larger-scale decarbonization efforts.                                                 |
| Source: Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory (DOE 2023c)           |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                 |

### Case Study and Communication

Further communication may include assembling and disseminating a case study on the project and/or having a poster in a public corridor of the building showcasing the project scope and GHG emissions savings. From there, communicating the environmental benefits of decarbonization to employees and customers can boost morale by demonstrating a commitment to reducing GHG emissions.

### Implementation

The University of California Santa Cruz is creating a Just Transition and Equity Subcommittee to conduct analysis around Diversity, Equity, Inclusion, and Justice while planning its campus decarbonization strategies. The subcommittee plans to use inclusive and community-centered approaches to ensure that decarbonization efforts align with community goals and needs.

### Additional Resources

For example, the University of California Santa Cruz is creating a Just Transition and Equity Subcommittee to conduct analysis around Diversity, Equity, Inclusion, and Justice while planning its campus decarbonization strategies. The subcommittee plans to use inclusive and community-centered approaches to ensure that decarbonization efforts align with community goals and needs.

### Implementation Example

The University of California Santa Cruz is creating a Just Transition and Equity Subcommittee to conduct analysis around Diversity, Equity, Inclusion, and Justice while planning its campus decarbonization strategies. The subcommittee plans to use inclusive and community-centered approaches to ensure that decarbonization efforts align with community goals and needs.

### Implementation Example

The University of California Santa Cruz is creating a Just Transition and Equity Subcommittee to conduct analysis around Diversity, Equity, Inclusion, and Justice while planning its campus decarbonization strategies. The subcommittee plans to use inclusive and community-centered approaches to ensure that decarbonization efforts align with community goals and needs.

# 3.3 Perform Decarbonization Assessment
Before implementing any decarbonization strategies, it is essential to conduct a decarbonization assessment to assess the building’s consumption and emissions patterns and identify areas for improvement. The assessment results in an energy audit report and a decarbonization plan. The plan is then used by facility management to guide future decisions on energy efficiency measures (EEM) and emissions reduction measures (ERM) implementation.

## 3.3.1 ASHRAE Energy Audit and Decarbonization Assessment
Energy audits define the baseline condition of the existing building and its systems and are performed to varying levels based on the need, budget, and timing. ANSI/ASHRAE/ACCA Standard 211 establishes industry practices for conducting and reporting energy audits for commercial buildings (ASHRAE 2023c). A decarbonization assessment includes all the steps of an energy audit and adds additional efficiency and emissions reductions measures, including electrification measures (even partial electrification solutions), fugitive emissions reduction measures, and further renewable energy measures. Depending on established targets and the determined project scope, the decarbonization assessment may also evaluate embodied carbon of the existing building and systems.

### Table 3.2: Primary Tasks Comparison
| Tasks                                                                     | Energy Audit | Decarbonization Assessment |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------- | -------------------------- |
| Pre-retrofit energy use analysis                                          |               |                           |

# GHG Emissions End-Use Balance
The GHG emissions end-use balance complements the existing energy end-use balance. The estimated emissions for each end use is calculated by multiplying the estimated energy for each end use by the corresponding GHG emission factors used to determine annual pre-retrofit GHG emissions.

## Table 3.3 Grid Emission Factor Databases

| Database | Description |
| --- | --- |
| U.S. EPA eGRID Database | The eGRID database includes annual historical GHG emission factors at the national, subregional, and utility-scale levels. |
| NREL Cambium Database | The National Renewable Energy Laboratory’s Cambium database provides modeled, forward-looking hourly emissions data for electricity generation across multiple grid scenarios. |
| UNFCCC Harmonized Grid Emission Factor Data Set | The United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change database lists grid emission factors outside of the United States divided by country/territory/island. The International Financial Institutions Technical Working Group on Greenhouse Gas Accounting (IFI TWG) periodically updates this data set. |
| Other International Emission Factors | Electricity Maps is an open-source tool showing carbon intensity (gCO eq/kWh) of electricity consumed throughout the world (Electricity Maps n.d.). The Gridwatch Web app includes live grid emissions for Ontario, Canada (Gridwatch n.d.). The Canadian government publishes Canadian emission factors by Province/Territory (Government of Canada n.d.). |

## Calculating Emissions

The estimated emissions for each end use is calculated by multiplying the estimated energy for each end use by the corresponding GHG emission factors used to determine annual pre-retrofit GHG emissions.

# Grid Emission Profiles
The emission profile of grid power generation is separate from building energy use. Such data may be obtained from an hourly modeled database, such as the NREL Cambium database, or from historical or projected utility-reported data. This type of profile informs how you might approach designing for lower carbon emissions by minimizing grid-supplied energy during peak grid emissions. Refer to Figure 3.5 for an example where the grid emission profile is marked as "marginal carbon."

# Building Emission Profiles
This type of profile combines the energy profile and the grid emission profile. It can be either building wide or end-use specific. Generally, the data are aggregated into annual values for the annual pre-retrofit GHG emissions or the end-use breakdowns, as described in the following section.

Any of the above profile types can also be combined on a single chart to provide further insights. For example, Figure 3.5 shows an example of the overlay of a nominal office building (with on-site PV) energy-use profile with the grid emission profile (marked as "marginal carbon").

The combination of energy-use profile and grid emission profile informs the selection of efficiency reduction measures compared to simply seeking energy efficiency measures. Periods when peak energy use coincides with peak grid emissions are the first targets for decarbonization. In the example building for Figure 3.8, the PV system reduces the energy load profile during mid-day hours, when the marginal emissions are lower. Further efficiencies during this mid-day period will have much less impact to the building's GHG emissions than addressing the peak areas, such as the morning warm-up period. In this example, one approach may be to incorporate thermal energy storage together with other measures for HVAC electrification to capture and store heat generated during the period of low GHG emissions (assisted by the on-site PV) for use during the morning warm-up period.

## Figure: Example overlay of a prototypical office's hourly load profile, marginal carbon rates, and peak TOU energy costs.
### Data Representation - Net building demand (with PV): 0 - 22 | Marginal carbon: 0 - 22 | High carbon and cost hours: 0 - 22
### Metric: Average hourly building demand vs. grid emission factors
### Chart Type: Line Chart
### Units: Hourly building demand vs. grid emission factors
### Chart Generation Hint
- X-axis: Hour
- Y-axis: Average hourly building demand vs. grid emission factors
- Net building demand (with PV): 0 - 22
- Marginal carbon: 0 - 22
- High carbon and cost hours: 0 - 22
- Baseline: 0
- Annotations: January

|                                                                                                                                                              |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| Figure 3.5 Example overlay of a prototypical office's hourly load profile, marginal carbo n
rates, and peak TOU energy costs. (Image credit: ASHRAE [2023d]) |

# Building Decarbonization Retrofits for Commercial and Multifamily Buildings

## Grid-Interactive Buildings for Decarbonization: Design and Operation Resource Guide

### 3.3.1.2 Heating and Cooling Load Profile Analysis

Heating and cooling load profile analysis is a key aspect of decarbonization projects and one that is not typically part of a standard energy audit. This type of analysis can identify HVAC or DHW systems that are oversized, presenting an opportunity to downsize, or rightsize, during the retrofit project. Opportunities to rightsize for heating load, in particular, can be critical when electrifying HVAC systems because, for many climates, the heating load will drive the heat pump capacity and sizing. Reducing heat pump capacity and size can be crucial when working within existing building constraints such as electrical capacity, structural capacity, and space. Additional details on rightsizing analysis are provided in Section 3.4.1.

Identifying periods of simultaneous heating and cooling, or thermal overlap, is another important aspect of load profile analysis. Where energy efficiency projects have traditionally focused on eliminating periods of thermal overlap, these periods represent prime opportunities for energy recovery within the building. Figure 3.6 illustrates the annual hourly heating and cooling loads for a building in red and blue, respectively, and then periods of simultaneous heating and cooling in yellow. In this figure, the building has weekday thermal overlap all year round, indicating a great opportunity for energy recovery.

## Figure: Building heating and cooling loads with thermal overlap.

Building heating and cooling loads with thermal overlap. The graph shows the annual hourly heating and cooling loads for a building in red and blue, respectively, and then periods of simultaneous heating and cooling in yellow. The building has weekday thermal overlap all year round, indicating a great opportunity for energy recovery. The x-axis represents the hours of the day, and the y-axis represents the kilowatts per hour (kW/h). The graph shows that the building has a high heating load during the winter months and a high cooling load during the summer months. The thermal overlap occurs during the weekdays, when the building is occupied, and is characterized by a high demand for both heating and cooling. This overlap presents an opportunity for energy recovery, as the building can utilize the waste heat from the cooling system to provide additional heating during the winter months.

|                                                                                                  |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| Figure 3.6 Building heating and cooling loads with thermal overlap. (Image credit: Smith-
Group) |

---

# Improvements to Building Operational Performance and Energy Use
### Reducing Thermal Loads and Demand
• Improvements to the building’s operational performance and energy use to reduce thermal loads, resulting in the downsizing of the HVAC equipment and reduction in demand and carbon emissions.

# Retrofitting Existing Equipment
### Decarbonizing Energy Sources
• Retrofitting or replacing existing equipment that uses fossil fuels with technologies that can use decarbonized energy, typically electricity.

# On-Site Renewable Energy
### Opportunities and Considerations
• Use of on-site renewable energy. While on-site renewable energy resource opportunities are introduced for Level 2 energy audits according to ANSI/ASHRAE/ACCA Standard 211, they should be investigated at all levels, and considerations should also include electric vehicle (EV) charging infrastructure and future parking space ventilation design.

# Energy Efficiency Measures and Renewable Measures
### In-Depth Discussion
More in-depth discussion on EEMs and ERMs is included in Section 3.4.

## Figure: Decarbonizing Building Thermal Systems: A Guide for Heat Pump Systems and Beyond

This figure is a book cover, which provides a comprehensive guide for heat pump systems and beyond. The guide focuses on how heat pumps should be applied and how they should be operated in commercial and multifamily buildings to support decarbonization.

### Data Representation

*   **Subtitle**: In conjunction with Design and Construction Allies
*   **Image**: A photo of a building with trees in the background
*   **Metric**: Decarbonization
*   **Chart Type**: Book Cover
*   **Units**: None

## Figure: Transitioning to Low-GWP Alternatives in Residential and Commercial Air Conditioning

This fact sheet provides current information on low global warming potential (GWP) alternative refrigerants to high-GWP hydrofluorocarbons (HFCs) for use in residential and commercial air conditioning (AC) equipment, including chillers (EPA 2016). HFCs are powerful greenhouse gases (GHGs) with GWPs hundreds to thousands of times more potent per pound than carbon dioxide (CO2); however, more low-GWP alternatives are becoming available.

### Data Representation

*   **Series/bar/line 1:** HFCs
*   **Series/bar/line 2:** Low-GWP alternatives
*   **Metric:** Global warming potential (GWP)
*   **Chart Type:** Bar chart
*   **Units:** GWPs per pound

### Chart Generation Hint

To recreate this chart, follow these steps:

*   **X-axis:** Label and categories: HFCs and low-GWP alternatives
*   **Y-axis:** Label and range: GWPs per pound
*   **Key threshold lines, annotations, or colour coding:** None
*   **Baseline or reference value:** None
*   **Refrigerant leakage should be included in the life-cycle emissions calculations for measures.**

# Framework for Greenhouse Gas Emissions Reduction Planning: Building Portfolios
The Department of Energy's Framework for Greenhouse Gas Emissions Reduction Planning: Building Portfolios provides guidance for reducing Scope 1 and Scope 2 GHG emissions for building portfolios and vehicle fleets (DOE 2023c). The framework includes a five-step process for developing an actionable plan for building owners and organizations, from establishing the building inventory to finalizing implementation scenarios, funding, phasing, etc.

## Figure: Framework for Greenhouse Gas Emissions Reduction Planning: Building Portfolios
The framework includes a five-step process for developing an actionable plan for building owners and organizations, from establishing the building inventory to finalizing implementation scenarios, funding, phasing, etc.

### Data Representation - Step 1: Building Inventory
- Metric: Building characteristics
- Chart Type: Diagram
- Units: None

### Data Representation - Step 2: Scenarios Development
- Metric: Emissions reduction scenarios
- Chart Type: Diagram
- Units: None

### Data Representation - Step 3: Funding and Phasing
- Metric: Funding and phasing plans
- Chart Type: Diagram
- Units: None

### Data Representation - Step 4: Implementation
- Metric: Implementation plans
- Chart Type: Diagram
- Units: None

### Data Representation - Step 5: Monitoring and Evaluation
- Metric: Monitoring and evaluation plans
- Chart Type: Diagram
- Units: None

### Chart Generation Hint
To recreate the figure, follow these steps:

- X-axis: Building characteristics (e.g., building type, size, location)
- Y-axis: Emissions reduction scenarios (e.g., energy efficiency measures, renewable energy sources)
- Baseline or reference value: Current emissions levels
- Color coding: Different colors for different building types or scenarios
- Annotations: Key findings and recommendations

Note: The exact chart generation hint is not provided as it is not possible to recreate the figure without additional information.

# Climate Zone Different technical approaches may apply based on climate zone (e.g., cold climate heat pumps and envelope measures). Select representative buildings in the range of climate zones in which the portfolio is located.

## Ownership Structure
Ownership/management/lease structures may result in differences in utility payment responsibility, so categorizing by ownership structure can be useful.

## Disadvantaged Communities
Determine if buildings are located within disadvantaged communities and consider these buildings for investment.

## Energy Prices
Prioritize buildings with the highest energy prices.

## Regulatory Drivers
Prioritize locations with building performance standards, benchmarking and decarbonization ordinances, strong building codes, and potential financial penalties or taxes.

## Utility Grid Carbon Intensity
Prioritize overall GHG emissions reduction efforts in regions with high grid carbon intensity and prioritize electrification in regions with lower grid carbon intensity.

## Utility Incentives and Funding
Prioritize locations with utility programs and grants that support opportunities electrification and building efficiency improvements.

### Source
Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory (DOE 2023c)

### Note
CO e/ft is carbon dioxide equivalent per square foot; kBtu/ft is thousands of British thermal units per square foot.

# Path for Existing Building Decarbonization

All rights reserved. No further reproduction or distribution is permitted. Distributed by Accuris for ASHRAE, www.accuristech.com. Copyrighted material licensed to Gunasekaran Raju on 2026-01-10 for licensee's use only.

## Figure: Framework for Building Decarbonization Assessment

This figure illustrates a framework for building decarbonization assessment, highlighting key steps and considerations. The framework is divided into several sections, including:

*   **Ongoing Assessment**: This section involves ongoing monitoring and evaluation of the building's energy performance and carbon emissions.
*   **Maximize Energy Efficiency**: This section focuses on identifying opportunities to reduce energy consumption through efficient systems and technologies.
*   **Reduce Electrical, Heating, and Cooling Loads**: This section involves reducing the demand for electricity, heating, and cooling by optimizing building operations and using energy-efficient equipment.
*   **Collect and Analyze Data**: This section emphasizes the importance of collecting and analyzing data to inform decision-making and identify areas for improvement.
*   **Building Upgrades**: This section involves upgrading building systems and components to improve energy efficiency and reduce emissions.
*   **Optimize Energy Performance**: This section focuses on optimizing energy performance through measures such as energy management systems and building automation.
*   **Optimize Energy Performance**: This section involves optimizing energy performance through measures such as energy management systems and building automation.
*   **Consider Embodied Carbon**: This section highlights the importance of considering embodied carbon in building materials and construction processes.
*   **Integrate Grid Interactivity and Microgrids**: This section involves integrating building energy systems with the grid and microgrids to optimize energy use and reduce emissions.
*   **Assess Distributed Generation and Clean Power Production**: This section involves assessing the potential for distributed generation and clean power production to reduce reliance on the grid and lower emissions.
*   **Evaluate Financial Considerations**: This section emphasizes the importance of evaluating financial considerations when implementing decarbonization measures.

## Data Representation

*   **Building System**: Building Lighting Systems
*   **Chapter/Section**: Section 5.1.1
*   **Building Appliances and Plug Loads**: Sections 5.1.2–5.1.3
*   **Building Envelope**: Section 5.2.1
*   **HVAC Energy Efficiency and Optimization**: Section 5.2.3
*   **HVAC Energy Recovery and Ventilation**: Sections 5.2.3.3–5.2.3.4

*   **X-axis**: Label and categories
*   **Y-axis**: Label and range
*   **Key Threshold Lines**: Identify key threshold lines or annotations
*   **Colour Coding**: Use colour coding to highlight important information
*   **Baseline or Reference Value**: Identify a baseline or reference value if applicable
